          <img alt="Map Italophone World.png" data-file-height="577" data-file-width="1365" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Map_Italophone_World.png/310px-Map_Italophone_World.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Map_Italophone_World.png/465px-Map_Italophone_World.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Map_Italophone_World.png/620px-Map_Italophone_World.png 2x" width="310"/>
         </a>
         <div style="text-align:left;">

      <p>
       <b>
        Italian
       </b>
       (
       <span class="unicode haudio">
        <span class="fn">
         <span style="white-space:nowrap">
          <a href="/wiki/File:It-italiano.ogg" title="About this sound">
           <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
          </a>
         </span>
         <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/It-italiano.ogg" title="It-italiano.ogg">
          <i>
           italiano
          </i>
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">
         (
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">
          <span style="cursor:help;">
           help
          </span>
         </a>
         ·
         <a href="/wiki/File:It-italiano.ogg" title="File:It-italiano.ogg">
          <span style="cursor:help;">
           info
          </span>
         </a>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">
         [itaˈljaːno]
        </a>
       </span>
       or
       <i>
        lingua italiana
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">
         [ˈliŋɡwa itaˈljaːna]
        </a>
       </span>
       ) is a
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance language
       </a>
       . By most measures, Italian, together with
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">
        Sardinian
       </a>
       , is the closest to
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       of the Romance languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Italian is an official language in
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
        Switzerland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Marino" title="San Marino">
        San Marino
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">
        Vatican City
       </a>
       and western
       <a href="/wiki/Istria" title="Istria">
        Istria
       </a>
       (in
       <a href="/wiki/Slovene_Istria" title="Slovene Istria">
        Slovenia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Istria_County" title="Istria County">
        Croatia
       </a>
       ). It used to have official status in
       <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">
        Albania
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">
        Malta
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">
        Monaco
       </a>
       , where it is still widely spoken, as well as in former
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_East_Africa" title="Italian East Africa">
        Italian East Africa
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_North_Africa" title="Italian North Africa">
        Italian North Africa
       </a>
       regions where it plays a significant role in various sectors. Italian is also spoken by large
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_diaspora" title="Italian diaspora">
        expatriate communities
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Americas" title="The Americas">
        the Americas
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnologue.com_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ethnologue.com-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has official minority status in
       <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">
        Bosnia and Herzegovina
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">
        Croatia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">
        Slovenia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">
        Romania
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many speakers are native bilinguals of both standardized Italian and
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
        other regional languages
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Italian is a major European language, being one of the official languages of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Security_and_Cooperation_in_Europe" title="Organisation for Security and Cooperation in Europe">
        Organisation for Security and Cooperation in Europe
       </a>
       and one of the working languages of the
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">
        Council of Europe
       </a>
       . It is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_EU" title="Languages of the EU">
        third most widely spoken first language in the European Union
       </a>
       with 65 million native speakers (13% of the EU population) and it is spoken as a second language by 14 million EU citizens (3%).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europa2006_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-europa2006-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Including Italian speakers in non-EU European countries (such as Switzerland and Albania) and on other continents, the total number of speakers is around 85 million.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Italian_language_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Italian_language-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Italian is the main working language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">
        Holy See
       </a>
       , serving as the
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       (common language) in the Roman Catholic hierarchy as well as the official language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_Military_Order_of_Malta" title="Sovereign Military Order of Malta">
        Sovereign Military Order of Malta
       </a>
       . Italian is known as the
       <i>
        language of music
       </i>
       because of its use in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_terminology" title="Musical terminology">
        musical terminology
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">
        opera
       </a>
       . Its influence is also widespread in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arts" title="Arts">
        arts
       </a>
       and in the
       <a href="/wiki/Luxury_goods" title="Luxury goods">
        luxury goods
       </a>
       market. Italian has been reported as the fourth or fifth most frequently taught foreign language in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-becomingitalianwordbyword.typepad.com_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-becomingitalianwordbyword.typepad.com-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Italian was adopted by the state after the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unification_of_Italy" title="Unification of Italy">
        Unification of Italy
       </a>
       , having previously been a literary language based on
       <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_dialect" title="Tuscan dialect">
        Tuscan
       </a>
       as spoken mostly by the
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_class" title="Upper class">
        upper class
       </a>
       of Florentine society.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its development was also influenced by other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_languages" title="Italian languages">
        Italian languages
       </a>
       and to some minor extent, by the
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic languages
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Migration_period" title="Migration period">
        post-Roman invaders
       </a>
       . The incorporation into Italian of learned, or "bookish" words from its own ancestor language,
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       , is arguably another form of lexical borrowing through the influence of written language and the liturgical language of the Church. Throughout the Middle Ages and into the early modern period, most literate Italian speakers were also literate in Latin; and thus they easily adopted Latin words into their writing—and eventually speech—in Italian. Its vowels are the second-closest to Latin after
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">
        Sardinian
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unlike most other Romance languages, Italian retains Latin's contrast between short and
       <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">
        long consonants
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As in most
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">
        stress
       </a>
       is distinctive.
      </p>
      <p>
       <span id="Middle_Ages">
       </span>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           needs additional citations for
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">

           </a>
          </b>

          <span class="hide-when-compact">

           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit">

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">
        Origins
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       During the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
        Middle Ages
       </a>
       , the established written language in Europe was Latin. With the great majority of people illiterate, however, only a handful were well versed in the language. In Italy, as in all other countries, the majority would instead speak the vernacular (native tongue) of their region. These dialects (as they are commonly referred to as) were derived from
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       over the course of centuries, evolving naturally unaffected by formal standards and teachings. These
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
        languages of Italy
       </a>
       are not truly "dialects" of Standard Italian, evolving independently (and alongside) of the predecessor of Standard Italian. They are often mutually unintelligible, and are better classified as distinct languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The standard Italian language has a poetic and literary origin in the writings of
       <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">
        Tuscan
       </a>
       writers of the 12th century, and, even though the grammar and core lexicon are basically unchanged from those used in Florence in the 13th century,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the modern standard of the language was largely shaped by relatively recent events. However, Italian as a language spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       and some surrounding regions has a longer history. In fact, the earliest surviving texts that can definitely be called Italian (or more accurately, vernacular, as distinct from its predecessor
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       ) are legal formulae known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Placiti_Cassinesi" title="Placiti Cassinesi">
        Placiti Cassinesi
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Benevento" title="Duchy of Benevento">
        Province of Benevento
       </a>
       that date from 960–963, although the
       <a href="/wiki/Veronese_Riddle" title="Veronese Riddle">
        Veronese Riddle
       </a>
       , probably from the 8th or early 9th century, contains a late form of Vulgar Latin that can be seen as a very early Italian dialect.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       What would come to be thought of as Italian was first formalized in the early 14th century through the works of Tuscan writer
       <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">
        Dante Alighieri
       </a>
       , written in his native
       <a href="/wiki/Florentine_dialect" title="Florentine dialect">
        Florentine
       </a>
       . Dante's epic poems, known collectively as the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">
         Commedia
        </a>
        ,
       </i>
       to which another Tuscan poet
       <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">
        Giovanni Boccaccio
       </a>
       later affixed the title
       <i>
        Divina
       </i>
       , were read throughout Italy and his written dialect became the "canonical standard" that all educated
       <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">
        Italians
       </a>
       could understand. Dante is still credited with standardizing the Italian language. In addition to the widespread exposure gained through literature, the Florentine language also gained prestige due to the political and cultural significance of Florence at the time and the fact that it was linguistically an intermediate between northern and southern dialects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus the dialect of
       <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">
        Florence
       </a>
       became the basis for what would become the official language of Italy.
      </p>
      <p>
       Italian often was an official language of the various Italian states predating unification, slowly replacing Latin, even when ruled by foreign powers (such as the Spanish in the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Naples" title="Kingdom of Naples">
        Kingdom of Naples
       </a>
       , or the Austrians in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Lombardy-Venetia" title="Kingdom of Lombardy-Venetia">
        Kingdom of Lombardy-Venetia
       </a>
       ), even though the masses spoke primarily vernacular languages and dialects. Italian was also one of the many recognised languages in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Empire" title="Austro-Hungarian Empire">
        Austro-Hungarian Empire
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Italy has always had a distinctive dialect for each city because the cities, until recently, were thought of as
       <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">
        city-states
       </a>
       . Those dialects now have considerable
       <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">
        variety
       </a>
       . As Tuscan-derived Italian came to be used throughout Italy, features of local speech were naturally adopted, producing various versions of Regional Italian. The most characteristic differences, for instance, between
       <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">
        Roman
       </a>
       Italian and
       <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">
        Milanese
       </a>
       Italian are the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consonant_length" title="Consonant length">
        gemination
       </a>
       of initial consonants and the pronunciation of stressed "e", and of "s" in some cases: e.g.
       <i>
        va bene
       </i>
       "all right": is pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [va ˈbːɛne]
       </span>
       by a Roman (and by any standard-speaker),
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [va ˈbene]
       </span>
       by a Milanese (and by any speaker whose native dialect lies to the north of the
       <a href="/wiki/La_Spezia%E2%80%93Rimini_Line" title="La Spezia–Rimini Line">
        La Spezia–Rimini Line
       </a>
       );
       <i>
        a casa
       </i>
       "at home" is
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [a ˈkːasa]
       </span>
       for Roman and standard,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [a ˈkaza]
       </span>
       for Milanese and generally northern.
      </p>
      <p>
       In contrast to the
       <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Italic_languages" title="Gallo-Italic languages">
        Gallo-Italic languages
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Italy" title="Northern Italy">
        northern Italy
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Italo-Dalmatian_languages" title="Italo-Dalmatian languages">
        Italo-Dalmatian
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Neapolitan_language" title="Neapolitan language">
        Neapolitan language
       </a>
       and its dialects were largely unaffected by the Franco-
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
        Occitan
       </a>
       influences introduced to Italy mainly by
       <a href="/wiki/Bard" title="Bard">
        bards
       </a>
       from France during the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
        Middle Ages
       </a>
       , but after the
       <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_southern_Italy" title="Norman conquest of southern Italy">
        Norman conquest of southern Italy
       </a>
       , Sicily became the first Italian land to adopt Occitan lyric moods (and words) in poetry. Even in the case of Northern Italian languages, however, scholars are careful not to overstate the effects of outsiders on the natural indigenous developments of the languages.
      </p>
      <p>
       The economic might and relatively advanced development of Tuscany at the time (
       <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">
        Late Middle Ages
       </a>
       ) gave its language weight, though the
       <a href="/wiki/Venetian_language" title="Venetian language">
        Venetian language
       </a>
       remained widespread in medieval Italian commercial life, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligurian_language_(Romance)" title="Ligurian language (Romance)">
        Ligurian (or Genoese)
       </a>
       remained in use in maritime trade alongside the Mediterranean. The increasing political and cultural relevance of
       <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">
        Florence
       </a>
       during the periods of the rise of the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medici_bank" title="Medici bank">
         Banco Medici
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">
        Humanism
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
        Renaissance
       </a>
       made its dialect, or rather a refined version of it, a standard in the arts.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance">
        Renaissance
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Renaissance">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
        Renaissance
       </a>
       era, known as
       <i>
        il Rinascimento
       </i>
       in Italian, was seen as a time of "rebirth", which is the literal meaning of both
       <i>
        renaissance
       </i>
       (from French) and
       <i>
        rinascimento
       </i>
       (Italian).
      </p>
      <p>
       During this time, long-existing beliefs stemming from the teachings of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">
        Roman Catholic Church
       </a>
       began to be understood from new a perspectives as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humanists" title="Humanists">
        humanists
       </a>
       —individuals who placed emphasis on the human body and its full potential—began to shift focus from the church to human beings themselves.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Humanists began forming new beliefs in various forms: social, political, and intellectual. The ideals of the Renaissance were evident throughout the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">
        Protestant Reformation
       </a>
       , which took place simultaneously with the Renaissance. The Protestant Reformation began with
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">
        Martin Luther
       </a>
       's rejection of the selling of indulgences by
       <a href="/wiki/Johann_Tetzel" title="Johann Tetzel">
        Johann Tetzel
       </a>
       and other authorities within the Roman Catholic Church, resulting in Luther's eventual break-off from the Roman Catholic Church in the
       <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Worms" title="Diet of Worms">
        Diet of Worms
       </a>
       . After Luther was excommunicated from the Roman Catholic Church, he founded what was then understood to be a sect of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">
        Catholicism
       </a>
       , later referred to as
       <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">
        Lutheranism
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Luther's preaching in favor of faith and scripture rather than tradition led him to translate the
       <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">
        Bible
       </a>
       into many other languages, which would allow for people from all over Europe to read the Bible. Previously, the Bible was only written in
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       , but after the Bible was translated, it could be understood in many other languages, including Italian. The Italian language was able to spread even more with the help of Luther and the invention of the
       <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">
        printing press
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">
        Johannes Gutenberg
       </a>
       . The printing press facilitated the spread of Italian because it was able to rapidly produce texts, such as the Bible, and cut the costs of books which allowed for more people to have access to the translated Bible and new pieces of literature.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Roman Catholic Church was losing its control over the population, as it was not open to change, and there was an increasing number of reformers with differing beliefs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_17-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:189px;max-width:189px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:187px;max-width:187px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dante_Luca.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1012" data-file-width="1024" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dante_Luca.jpg/185px-Dante_Luca.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dante_Luca.jpg/278px-Dante_Luca.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dante_Luca.jpg/370px-Dante_Luca.jpg 2x" width="185"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:187px;max-width:187px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Altichiero,_ritratto_di_Francesco_Petrarca.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="2088" data-file-width="1772" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Altichiero%2C_ritratto_di_Francesco_Petrarca.jpg/185px-Altichiero%2C_ritratto_di_Francesco_Petrarca.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Altichiero%2C_ritratto_di_Francesco_Petrarca.jpg/278px-Altichiero%2C_ritratto_di_Francesco_Petrarca.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Altichiero%2C_ritratto_di_Francesco_Petrarca.jpg/370px-Altichiero%2C_ritratto_di_Francesco_Petrarca.jpg 2x" width="185"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">
           Dante Alighieri
          </a>
          (
          <i>
           top
          </i>
          ) and
          <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">
           Petrarch
          </a>
          (
          <i>
           bottom
          </i>
          ) were influential in establishing their
          <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_dialect" title="Tuscan dialect">
           Tuscan dialect
          </a>
          as the most prominent literary language in all of
          <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
           Italy
          </a>
          in the
          <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">
           Late Middle Ages
          </a>
          .
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pietro_Bembo2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="975" data-file-width="764" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pietro_Bembo2.jpg/140px-Pietro_Bembo2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pietro_Bembo2.jpg/210px-Pietro_Bembo2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pietro_Bembo2.jpg/280px-Pietro_Bembo2.jpg 2x" width="140"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pietro_Bembo2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Bembo" title="Pietro Bembo">
          Pietro Bembo
         </a>
         was an influential figure in the development of the Italian language from the Tuscan dialect, as a literary medium, codifying the language for standard modern usage.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Italian became the language used in the courts of every state in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_peninsula" title="Italian peninsula">
        Italian peninsula
       </a>
       . The rediscovery of Dante's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/De_vulgari_eloquentia" title="De vulgari eloquentia">
         De vulgari eloquentia
        </a>
       </i>
       and a renewed interest in linguistics in the 16th century, sparked a debate that raged throughout Italy concerning the criteria that should govern the establishment of a modern Italian literary and spoken language. This discussion, known as
       <i>
        "questione della lingua"
       </i>
       (i. e., the
       <i>
        problem of the language
       </i>
       ), ran through the Italian culture until the end of the 19th century, often linked to the political debate on achieving a united Italian state. Renaissance scholars divided into three main factions:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purism_(language)" title="Purism (language)">
         purists
        </a>
        , headed by Venetian
        <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Bembo" title="Pietro Bembo">
         Pietro Bembo
        </a>
        (who, in his
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Gli_Asolani" title="Gli Asolani">
          Gli Asolani
         </a>
        </i>
        , claimed the language might be based only on the great literary classics, such as
        <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">
         Petrarch
        </a>
        and some part of Boccaccio). The purists thought the Divine Comedy was not dignified enough because it used elements from non-lyric registers of the language.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Machiavelli" title="Niccolò Machiavelli">
         Niccolò Machiavelli
        </a>
        and other
        <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">
         Florentines
        </a>
        preferred the version spoken by ordinary people in their own times.
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Courtier" title="Courtier">
         courtiers
        </a>
        , like
        <a href="/wiki/Baldassare_Castiglione" title="Baldassare Castiglione">
         Baldassare Castiglione
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Gian_Giorgio_Trissino" title="Gian Giorgio Trissino">
         Gian Giorgio Trissino
        </a>
        , insisted that each local vernacular contribute to the new standard.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       A fourth faction claimed the best Italian was the one that the papal court adopted, which was a mix of Florentine and the dialect of Rome. Eventually, Bembo's ideas prevailed, and the foundation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Accademia_della_Crusca" title="Accademia della Crusca">
        Accademia della Crusca
       </a>
       in Florence (1582–1583), the official legislative body of the Italian language led to publication of
       <a href="/wiki/Agnolo_Monosini" title="Agnolo Monosini">
        Agnolo Monosini
       </a>
       's Latin tome
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Floris_Italicae_lingue_libri_novem" title="Floris Italicae lingue libri novem">
         Floris italicae linguae libri novem
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1604 followed by the first Italian dictionary in 1612.
      </p>
      <p>
       The continual advancements in technology plays a crucial role in the diffusion of languages. After the invention of the
       <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">
        printing press
       </a>
       in the fifteen century, the number of printing presses in Italy grew rapidly and by the year 1500 reached a total of 56, the biggest number of printing presses in all of Europe. This allowed to produce more pieces of literature at a lower cost and as the dominant language, Italian spread.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">
        Modern era
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern era">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       An important event that helped the diffusion of Italian was the conquest and occupation of Italy by
       <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">
        Napoleon
       </a>
       in the early 19th century (who was himself of Italian-Corsican descent). This conquest propelled the unification of Italy some decades after and pushed the Italian language into a
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       used not only among clerks, nobility, and functionaries in the Italian courts, but also by the
       <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">
        bourgeoisie
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_times">
        Contemporary times
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Contemporary times">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Italian literature's first modern novel,
       <a href="/wiki/The_Betrothed_(Manzoni_novel)" title="The Betrothed (Manzoni novel)">
        <i>
         I Promessi Sposi
        </i>
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        The Betrothed
       </i>
       ), by
       <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Manzoni" title="Alessandro Manzoni">
        Alessandro Manzoni
       </a>
       , further defined the standard by "rinsing" his Milanese "in the waters of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arno_River" title="Arno River">
        Arno
       </a>
       " (
       <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">
        Florence
       </a>
       's river), as he states in the Preface to his 1840 edition.
      </p>
      <p>
       After unification, a huge number of civil servants and soldiers recruited from all over the country introduced many more words and idioms from their home languages ("
       <a href="/wiki/Ciao" title="Ciao">
        ciao
       </a>
       " is derived from
       <a href="/wiki/Venetian_language" title="Venetian language">
        Venetian
       </a>
       word "s-cia[v]o" (
       <i>
        slave
       </i>
       ), "
       <a href="/wiki/Panettone" title="Panettone">
        panettone
       </a>
       " comes from
       <a href="/wiki/Lombard_language" title="Lombard language">
        Lombard
       </a>
       word "panetton" etc.). Only 2.5% of Italy's population could speak the Italian standardized language properly when the nation was unified in 1861.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Italian is a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">
        Romance language
       </a>
       , and is therefore a descendant of
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       (the spoken form of non-classical Latin).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [note 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Standard Italian is based on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuscan_language" title="Tuscan language">
        Tuscan
       </a>
       , especially its
       <a href="/wiki/Florentine_dialect" title="Florentine dialect">
        Florentine dialect
       </a>
       , and is therefore an
       <a href="/wiki/Italo-Dalmatian_languages" title="Italo-Dalmatian languages">
        Italo-Dalmatian language
       </a>
       , to which
       <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_language" title="Sicilian language">
        Sicilian
       </a>
       and the extinct
       <a href="/wiki/Dalmatian_language" title="Dalmatian language">
        Dalmatian
       </a>
       also belong, among a few others.
      </p>
      <p>
       Unlike most other Romance languages, Italian retains Latin's contrast between short and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consonant_length" title="Consonant length">
        long consonants
       </a>
       . As in most
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">
        stress
       </a>
       is distinctive. In particular, among the Romance languages, Italian is the closest to Latin in terms of
       <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">
        vocabulary
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grimes_1996_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grimes_1996-26">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Lexical_similarity" title="Lexical similarity">
        Lexical similarity
       </a>
       is 89% with
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       , 87% with
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       , 85% with
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">
        Sardinian
       </a>
       , 82% with
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       , 78% with
       <a href="/wiki/Ladin_language" title="Ladin language">
        Ladin
       </a>
       , and 77% with
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
        Romanian
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnologue.com_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ethnologue.com-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MED_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MED-27">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ezglot.com_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ezglot.com-28">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       One study analyzing the degree of differentiation of Romance languages in comparison to Latin (comparing
       <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">
        phonology
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">
        inflection
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">
        discourse
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">
        syntax
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">
        vocabulary
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Intonation_(linguistics)" title="Intonation (linguistics)">
        intonation
       </a>
       ) estimated that among the languages analyzed the distance between Italian and Latin is only higher than that between Sardinian and Latin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">
        Geographic distribution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Idioma_italiano.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="464" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Idioma_italiano.png/200px-Idioma_italiano.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Idioma_italiano.png/300px-Idioma_italiano.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Idioma_italiano.png/400px-Idioma_italiano.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Idioma_italiano.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Use of the Italian language in Europe
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lengua_italiana.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="522" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Lengua_italiana.png/200px-Lengua_italiana.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Lengua_italiana.png/300px-Lengua_italiana.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Lengua_italiana.png/400px-Lengua_italiana.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lengua_italiana.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Use of the Italian language in Europe and former use in Africa
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">
        Europe
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Europe">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Italian is an official language of
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/San_Marino" title="San Marino">
        San Marino
       </a>
       and is spoken fluently by the majority of the countries' populations. Italian is official, together with French, German and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romansch_language" title="Romansch language">
        Romansch
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
        Switzerland
       </a>
       , with most of the 0.5 million speakers concentrated in the south of the country, in the cantons of
       <a href="/wiki/Ticino" title="Ticino">
        Ticino
       </a>
       and southern
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graub%C3%BCnden" title="Graubünden">
        Graubünden
       </a>
       (predominately in
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_Graub%C3%BCnden" title="Italian Graubünden">
        Italian Grigioni
       </a>
       ). Italian is the third most spoken language in Switzerland (after German and French), and its use has modestly declined since the 1970s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-offstat_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-offstat-30">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Italian is also used in administration and official documents in
       <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">
        Vatican City
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Italian is widely spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">
        Malta
       </a>
       , where nearly two-thirds of the population can speak it fluently.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Europoll_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Europoll-32">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Italian served as Malta's official language until 1934, when it was abolished by the British colonial administration amid strong local opposition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Italian is also recognized as an official language in
       <a href="/wiki/Istria_County" title="Istria County">
        Istria County
       </a>
       , Croatia, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slovenian_Istria" title="Slovenian Istria">
        Slovenian Istria
       </a>
       , where there are significant and historic Italian populations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It is used as the official language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_Military_Order_of_Malta" title="Sovereign Military Order of Malta">
        Sovereign Military Order of Malta
       </a>
       , a Roman Catholic chivalric order which, while not a nation per se, is still recognized as a sovereign subject of international law.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">
        Albania
       </a>
       , it is one of the most spoken languages. This is due to the strong historical ties between
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">
        Albania
       </a>
       but also the Albanian communities in Italy, and the 19,000
       <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">
        Italians
       </a>
       living in Albania.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is reported as high as 70% of the Albanian adult population has some form of knowledge of Italian. Furthermore, the Albanian government has pushed to make Italian a compulsory second language in schools.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, Italian is the third most spoken language in the country after
       <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">
        Albanian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Italian is also spoken by a minority in
       <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">
        Monaco
       </a>
       and France (especially in the southeast region of the country).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008census_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-2008census-39">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">
        Africa
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Africa">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Due to heavy Italian influence during the
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_Empire" title="Italian Empire">
        Italian colonial period
       </a>
       , Italian is still understood by some in former colonies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnologue.com_8-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ethnologue.com-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although it was the primary language in
       <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">
        Libya
       </a>
       since
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_Libya" title="Italian Libya">
        colonial rule
       </a>
       , Italian greatly declined under the
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi" title="History of Libya under Muammar Gaddafi">
        rule of Muammar Gaddafi
       </a>
       , who expelled the
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_settlers_in_Libya" title="Italian settlers in Libya">
        Italian Libyan
       </a>
       population and made
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_Arabic" title="Literary Arabic">
        Arabic
       </a>
       the sole official language of the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless, Italian continues to be used in economic sectors in Libya. In Eritrea, Italian is at times used in commerce and the capital city
       <a href="/wiki/Asmara" title="Asmara">
        Asmara
       </a>
       still has one Italian-language school.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Italian was also introduced to
       <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">
        Somalia
       </a>
       through colonialism and was the sole official language of administration and education during the
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_Somaliland" title="Italian Somaliland">
        colonial period
       </a>
       but fell out of use after government, educational and economic infrastructure were destroyed in the
       <a href="/wiki/Somali_Civil_War" title="Somali Civil War">
        Somali Civil War
       </a>
       . Italian is still understood by some elderly and other people. The official languages of the Somali Republic are
       <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">
        Somali
       </a>
       (Maay and Maxaatiri) and Arabic. The working languages during the Transitional Federal Government were Italian and English.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Immigrant_communities">
        Immigrant communities
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Immigrant communities">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Although over 17 million
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_American" title="Italian American">
        Americans are of Italian descent
       </a>
       , only a little over one million people in the United States speak Italian at home.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless, an Italian language media market does exist in the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On the other hand, although technology allows for the Italian language to spread globally, there has been a decrease in the number of Italian speakers in the United States. According to the U.S. Census Bureau, the number of Italian speakers in 1980 was 1,614,344. In 1990, the number of Italian speakers in the United States dropped to 1,308,648. In 2000, the number of speakers decreased to 1,008,370, and finally, in 2010, the number of Italian speakers plummeted to 725,223. The percent change from 1980–2010 was a negative 55.2.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
        Canada
       </a>
       , Italian is the second most spoken non-official language when
       <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">
        varieties of Chinese
       </a>
       are not grouped together, with over 660,000 speakers (or about 2.1% of the population) according to the 2006 Census.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statistics_Canada_2006_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Statistics_Canada_2006-47">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       , Italian is the second most spoken foreign language after Chinese, with 1.4% of the population speaking it as their home language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Italian immigrants to
       <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">
        South America
       </a>
       have also brought a presence of the language to that continent. Italian is the second most spoken language in
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       after the official language of Spanish, with over 1 million (mainly of the older generation) speaking it at home, and Italian has also influenced the dialect of Spanish spoken in Argentina and
       <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">
        Uruguay
       </a>
       , mostly in phonology, as well as the Portuguese
       <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">
        prosody
       </a>
       of the Brazilian state of
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       which itself has 15 million Italian descendants. This form of Spanish is known as
       <a href="/wiki/Rioplatense_Spanish" title="Rioplatense Spanish">
        Rioplatense Spanish
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Italian bilingual speakers can be found in the Southeast of
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       as well as in the South. In
       <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">
        Venezuela
       </a>
       , Italian is the second most spoken language after Spanish, with around 200,000 speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Smaller Italian-speaking minorities on the continent are also found in
       <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">
        Paraguay
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">
        Ecuador
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">
        Costa Rica
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">
        Central America
       </a>
       , Italian is one of the most important immigration community languages, after
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       . It is spoken in the southern area of the country in cities like
       <a href="/wiki/San_Vito_(Costa_Rica)" title="San Vito (Costa Rica)">
        San Vito
       </a>
       and other communities of Coto Brus, near the south borderline with
       <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">
        Panama
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Number_of_speakers_by_country.5B8.5D">
