      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
        Education
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Education">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Knowledge_of_Italian_EU_map.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="686" data-file-width="653" height="368" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Knowledge_of_Italian_EU_map.svg/350px-Knowledge_of_Italian_EU_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Knowledge_of_Italian_EU_map.svg/525px-Knowledge_of_Italian_EU_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Knowledge_of_Italian_EU_map.svg/700px-Knowledge_of_Italian_EU_map.svg.png 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Knowledge_of_Italian_EU_map.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Knowledge of Italian according to EU statistics
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Italian is widely taught in many schools around the world, but rarely as the first foreign language. Italian is the fourth
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-becomingitalianwordbyword.typepad.com_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-becomingitalianwordbyword.typepad.com-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       most frequently taught foreign language in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 21st century, technology also allows for the continual spread of the Italian language, as people have new ways for one to learn how to speak, read, and write languages at their own pace and at any given time. For example, in 2017 the free website and application
       <a href="/wiki/Duolingo" title="Duolingo">
        Duolingo
       </a>
       had 22.3 million English speakers learning the Italian language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       According to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs" title="Italian Ministry of Foreign Affairs">
        Italian Ministry of Foreign Affairs
       </a>
       , every year there are more than 200,000 foreign students who study the Italian language; they are distributed among the 90
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Cultural_Institute" title="Italian Cultural Institute">
        Institutes of Italian Culture
       </a>
       that are located around the world, in the 179 Italian schools located abroad, or in the 111 Italian lecturer sections belonging to foreign schools where Italian is taught as a language of culture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esteri.it_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-esteri.it-56">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the United States, Italian is the fourth most taught foreign language after Spanish, French, and German, in that order (or the fifth if
       <a href="/wiki/American_Sign_Language" title="American Sign Language">
        American Sign Language
       </a>
       is considered).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In central-east Europe Italian is first in Montenegro, second in Austria, Croatia, Slovenia, and Ukraine after English, and third in Hungary, Romania and Russia after English and German.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esteri.it_56-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-esteri.it-56">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       But throughout the world, Italian is the fifth most taught foreign language, after English, French, German, and Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       statistics, Italian is spoken as a native language by 13% of the EU population, or 65 million people,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europa2006_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-europa2006-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       mainly in Italy. In the EU, it is spoken as a second language by 3% of the EU population, or 14 million people. Among EU states, the percentage of people able to speak Italian well enough to have a conversation is 66% in
       <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">
        Malta
       </a>
       , 15% in
       <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">
        Slovenia
       </a>
       , 14% in
       <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">
        Croatia
       </a>
       , 8% in
       <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
        Austria
       </a>
       , 5% in
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">
        Luxembourg
       </a>
       , and 4% in the former
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">
        West Germany
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">
        Greece
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
        Cyprus
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">
        Romania
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Italian is also one of the national languages of Switzerland, which is not a part of the European Union.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Italian language is well-known and studied in
       <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">
        Albania
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       another non-EU member, due to its historical ties and geographical proximity to Italy and to the diffusion of Italian television in the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_and_derived_languages">
        Influence and derived languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Influence and derived languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_diaspora" title="Italian diaspora">
        Italian diaspora
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       From the late nineteenth to the mid-twentieth century, thousands of Italians settled in Argentina, Uruguay, southern Brazil, and Venezuela, where they formed a physical and cultural presence.
      </p>
      <p>
       In some cases, colonies were established where variants of regional
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
        languages of Italy
       </a>
       were used, and some continue to use this regional language. Examples are
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">
        Rio Grande do Sul
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       , where
       <a href="/wiki/Talian_dialect" title="Talian dialect">
        Talian
       </a>
       is used, and the town of
       <a href="/wiki/Chipilo" title="Chipilo">
        Chipilo
       </a>
       near Puebla,
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       ; each continues to use a derived form of
       <a href="/wiki/Venetian_language" title="Venetian language">
        Venetian
       </a>
       dating back to the nineteenth century. Another example is
       <a href="/wiki/Cocoliche" title="Cocoliche">
        Cocoliche
       </a>
       , an Italian–Spanish
       <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">
        pidgin
       </a>
       once spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       and especially in
       <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">
        Buenos Aires
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Lunfardo" title="Lunfardo">
        Lunfardo
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Rioplatense_Spanish" title="Rioplatense Spanish">
        Rioplatense Spanish
       </a>
       , and particularly the speech of the city of Buenos Aires, has intonation patterns that resemble those of
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
        Italian languages
       </a>
       because Argentina has had a continuous large influx of Italian settlers since the second half of the nineteenth century: initially primarily from northern Italy; then, since the beginning of the twentieth century, mostly from southern Italy.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lingua_franca">
        Lingua franca
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Lingua franca">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Lingua_Franca" title="Mediterranean Lingua Franca">
        Mediterranean Lingua Franca
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Starting in late
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">
        medieval
       </a>
       times in much of Europe and the Mediterranean, Latin was replaced as the primary commercial language by Italian language variants (especially Tuscan and Venetian). These variants were consolidated during the
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
        Renaissance
       </a>
       with the strength of Italy and the rise of
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">
        humanism
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/The_arts" title="The arts">
        the arts
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       During that period, Italy held artistic sway over the rest of Europe. It was the norm for all educated gentlemen to make the
       <a href="/wiki/Grand_Tour" title="Grand Tour">
        Grand Tour
       </a>
       , visiting Italy to see its great historical monuments and works of art. It thus became expected to learn at least some Italian. In England, while the classical languages
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       were the first to be learned, Italian became the second most common modern language after French, a position it held until the late eighteenth century, when it tended to be replaced by German.
       <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">
        John Milton
       </a>
       , for instance, wrote some of his early poetry in Italian.
      </p>
      <p>
       Within the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_church" title="Catholic church">
        Catholic church
       </a>
       , Italian is known by a large part of the ecclesiastical hierarchy, and is used in substitution for Latin in some official documents.
      </p>
      <p>
       Italian
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       continue to be used in most languages in matters of art and
       <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">
        music
       </a>
       (especially
       <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">
        opera
       </a>
       ), in the
       <a href="/wiki/Design" title="Design">
        design
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fashion" title="Fashion">
        fashion
       </a>
       industries, in some sports like
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Football_(association)" title="Football (association)">
        football
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and especially, in culinary terms.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">
        Dialects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dialects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Regional_Italian" title="Regional Italian">
        Regional Italian
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
        Languages of Italy
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Italy_-_Forms_of_Dialect.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="950" data-file-width="814" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Italy_-_Forms_of_Dialect.jpg/220px-Italy_-_Forms_of_Dialect.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Italy_-_Forms_of_Dialect.jpg/330px-Italy_-_Forms_of_Dialect.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Italy_-_Forms_of_Dialect.jpg/440px-Italy_-_Forms_of_Dialect.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Italy_-_Forms_of_Dialect.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Geographical distribution of major Italian dialects
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Throughout Italy, regional variations of Standard Italian, called
       <a href="/wiki/Regional_Italian" title="Regional Italian">
        Regional Italian
       </a>
       , are spoken. In Italy, almost all
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
        Romance languages spoken as the vernacular
       </a>
       —other than standard Italian and distantly-related, non-Romance languages spoken in border regions or among immigrant communities—are often imprecisely called "
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
        Italian dialects
       </a>
       ",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       even though they are quite different, with some belonging to different branches of the
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance language family
       </a>
       . The only exceptions to this are
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">
        Sardinian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ladin_language" title="Ladin language">
        Ladin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Friulian_language" title="Friulian language">
        Friulian
       </a>
       , which are officially recognized as distinct
       <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">
        minority languages
       </a>
       by the law. On the other hand,
       <a href="/wiki/Corsican_language" title="Corsican language">
        Corsican
       </a>
       (a language spoken on the
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        French
       </a>
       island of
       <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">
        Corsica
       </a>
       ) is closely related to
       <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_dialect" title="Tuscan dialect">
        Tuscan
       </a>
       , from which Standard Italian derives and evolved.
      </p>
      <p>
       The differences in the evolution of Latin in the different regions of Italy can be attributed to the presence of three other types of languages:
       <a href="/wiki/Stratum_(linguistics)" title="Stratum (linguistics)">
        substrata, superstrata, and adstrata
       </a>
       . The most prevalent were substrata (the language of the original inhabitants), as the Italian dialects were most likely simply Latin as spoken by native cultural groups. Superstrata and adstrata were both less important. Foreign conquerors of Italy that dominated different regions at different times left behind little to no influence on the dialects. Foreign cultures with which Italy engaged in peaceful relations with, such as trade, had no significant influence either.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_17-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Regional differences can be recognized by various factors: the openness of vowels, the length of the consonants, and influence of the local language (for example, in informal situations the contraction
       <i>
        <span lang="it">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ann%C3%A0#Italian" title="wikt:annà">
          annà
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       replaces
       <i>
        <span lang="it">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/andare#Italian" title="wikt:andare">
          andare
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       in the area of Rome for the infinitive "to go"; and
       <i>
        <span lang="it">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nare#Italian" title="wikt:nare">
          nare
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       is what
       <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">
        Venetians
       </a>
       say for the infinitive "to go").
      </p>
      <p>
       There is no definitive date when the various Italian variants of Latin—including varieties that contributed to modern Standard Italian—began to be distinct enough from Latin to be considered separate languages. From a linguistic perspective, two language variants are considered separate languages (rather than variant dialects of a single language) when they are no longer
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" title="Mutually intelligible">
        mutually intelligible
       </a>
       . For the Italian Romance languages, the first extant written evidence of varieties that can be considered no longer to be Latin comes from the ninth and tenth centuries C.E. These written sources demonstrate certain vernacular characteristics and sometimes explicitly mention the use of the vernacular in Italy. Full literary manifestations of the vernacular began to surface around the 13th century in the form of various religious texts and poetry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_17-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although these are the first written records of Italian varieties separate from Latin, the spoken language had likely diverged long before the first written records appear, since those who were literate generally wrote in Latin even if they spoke other Romance varieties in person.
      </p>
      <p>
       Throughout the 19th and 20th centuries, the use of Standard Italian became increasingly widespread and was mirrored by a decline in the use of the dialects. An increase in literacy was one of the main driving factors (one can assume that only literates were capable of learning Standard Italian, whereas those who were illiterate had access only to their native dialect). The percentage of literates rose from 25% in 1861 to 60% in 1911, and then on to 78.1% in 1951.
       <a href="/wiki/Tullio_De_Mauro" title="Tullio De Mauro">
        Tullio De Mauro
       </a>
       , an Italian linguist, has asserted that in 1861 only 2.5% of the population of Italy could speak Standard Italian. He reports that in 1951 that percentage had risen to 87%. The ability to speak Italian did not necessarily mean it was in everyday use, and most people (63.5%) still usually spoke their native dialects. In addition, other factors such as mass emigration, industrialization and urbanization, and internal migrations after
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       contributed to the proliferation of Standard Italian. The Italians who emigrated during the
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_diaspora" title="Italian diaspora">
        Italian diaspora
       </a>
       beginning in 1861 were often of the uneducated lower class, and thus the emigration had the effect of increasing the percentage of literates, who often knew and understood the importance of Standard Italian, back home in Italy. A large percentage of those who had emigrated also eventually returned to Italy, often more educated than when they had left.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_17-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Italian dialects have declined in the modern era, as Italy unified under Standard Italian and continues to do so aided by mass media, from newspapers to radios to television.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_17-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_phonology" title="Italian phonology">
        Italian phonology
       </a>
      </div>

          Trill
         </a>
        </th>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td style="border-right: 0;">
        </td>
        <td style="border-left: 0;">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
           r
          </a>

        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
       </tr>

      <p>
       Notes:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Between two vowels, or between a vowel and an approximant or liquid (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /l r/
        </span>
        or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /w j/
        </span>
        ), consonants can be either single or
        <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">
         geminated
        </a>
        . Geminated consonants shorten the preceding vowel (or block phonetic lengthening) and the first geminated element is
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Release_(phonetics)" title="Release (phonetics)">
         unreleased
        </a>
        . For example,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /fato/ [ˈfaː.to] ~ /fatto/ [ˈfat.to]
        </span>
        (first one means "fate, destiny" and the second means "fact", see "
        <i>
         <span lang="it">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fato#Italian" title="wikt:fato">
           fato
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        " and "
        <i>
         <span lang="it">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fatto#Italian" title="wikt:fatto">
           fatto
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        "). However,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɲ/, /ʃ/, /ʎ/,
        </span>
        are always geminated word-internally.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
         <a href="#cite_note-65">
          [64]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Similarly, nasals, liquids, and sibilants are pronounced slightly longer before medial consonant clusters.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
         <a href="#cite_note-66">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /z/
        </span>
        is the only consonant that cannot be geminated.
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /t d t͡s d͡z s z/
        </span>
        are denti-alveolar, while
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /l n/
        </span>
        are alveolar.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
         <a href="#cite_note-67">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
         <a href="#cite_note-68">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The trill
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /r/
        </span>
        is sometimes reduced to a single vibration when not geminated, but it is not a
        <a href="/wiki/Flap_consonant" title="Flap consonant">
         flap
        </a>
        *
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɾ]
        </span>
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
           <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2014)">
            clarification needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Nasals
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">
         assimilate
        </a>
        to the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Point_of_articulation" title="Point of articulation">
         point of articulation
        </a>
        of whatever consonant they precede. For example,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /nɡ/
        </span>
        is realized as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ŋɡ]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The distinction between
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /z/
        </span>
        is neutralized before consonants and at the beginning of words: the former is used before voiceless consonants and before vowels at the beginning of words; the latter is used before voiced consonants (meaning
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [z]
        </span>
        is an allophone of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
        before voiced consonants). The two are only contrasted between two vowels within a word. According to Canepari,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Handbook_of_Pronunciation_69-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-A_Handbook_of_Pronunciation-69">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
        though, the
        <i>
         traditional
        </i>
        standard has been replaced by a modern
        <i>
         neutral pronunciation
        </i>
        which always prefers
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /z/
        </span>
        when intervocalic, except when the intervocalic
        <i>
         s
        </i>
        is the initial sound of a word or a morpheme, if the compound is still felt as such: for example,
        <i>
         <span lang="it">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/presento#Italian" title="wikt:presento">
           presento
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /preˈsɛnto/
        </span>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
         <a href="#cite_note-70">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
        ('I foresee', with
        <i>
         pre
        </i>
        meaning 'before' and
        <i>
         sento
        </i>
        meaning 'I see') vs.
        <i>
         presento
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /preˈzɛnto/
        </span>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
         <a href="#cite_note-71">
          [70]
         </a>
        </sup>
        ('I present'). There are many words in which dictionaries now indicate that both pronunciations with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /z/
        </span>
        and with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
        are acceptable. The two phonemes have merged in many regional varieties of Italian, either into
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /z/
        </span>
        (Northern-Central) or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
        (Southern-Central). Geminate
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ss/
        </span>
        can be pronounced as single
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [s]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Italian has a seven-vowel system, consisting of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a, ɛ, e, i, ɔ, o, u/
       </span>
       , as well as 23 consonants. Compared with most other Romance languages, Italian phonology is conservative, preserving many words nearly unchanged from
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       . Some examples:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Italian
        <i>
         <span lang="it">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/quattordici#Italian" title="wikt:quattordici">
           quattordici
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        "fourteen" &lt; Latin
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         <i>
          <span lang="la">
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/quattuordecim#Latin" title="wikt:quattuordecim">
            quattuordecim
           </a>
          </span>
         </i>
        </span>
        (cf. Romanian
        <i>
         <span lang="ro">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/paisprezece#Romanian" title="wikt:paisprezece">
           paisprezece
          </a>
         </span>
         /
         <span lang="ro">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pai%C8%99pe#Romanian" title="wikt:paișpe">
           paișpe
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         <span lang="es">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/catorce#Spanish" title="wikt:catorce">
           catorce
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        , French
        <i>
         <span lang="fr">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/quatorze#French" title="wikt:quatorze">
           quatorze
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /kaˈtɔʁz/
        </span>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
         Catalan
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
         Portuguese
        </a>
        <i>
         <span lang="pt">
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/catorze" title="wikt:catorze">
           catorze
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Italian
        <i>
         settimana
        </i>
        "week" &lt; Latin
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         septimāna
        </span>
        (cf. Romanian
        <i>
         săptămână
        </i>
        , Spanish and Portuguese
        <i>
         semana
        </i>
        , French
        <i>
         semaine
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s(ǝ)ˈmɛn/
        </span>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
         Catalan
        </a>
        <i>
         setmana
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Italian
        <i>
         medesimo
        </i>
        "same" &lt; Vulgar Latin *
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         medi(p)simum
        </span>
        (cf. Spanish
        <i>
         mismo
        </i>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
         Portuguese
        </a>
        <i>
         mesmo
        </i>
        , French
        <i>
         même
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /mɛm/
        </span>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
         Catalan
        </a>
        <i>
         mateix
        </i>
        ; note that Italian usually uses the shorter
        <i>
         stesso
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Italian
        <i>
         guadagnare
        </i>
        "to win, earn, gain" &lt; Vulgar Latin *
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         guadanyāre
        </span>
        &lt;
        <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
         Germanic
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /waidanjan/
        </span>
        (cf. Spanish
        <i>
         ganar
        </i>
        , Portuguese
        <i>
         ganhar
        </i>
        , French
        <i>
         gagner
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɡaˈɲe/
        </span>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
         Catalan
        </a>
        <i>
         guanyar
        </i>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The conservativeness of Italian phonology is partly explained by its origin. Italian stems from a literary language that is derived from the 13th-century speech of the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">
        Florence
       </a>
       in the region of
       <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">
        Tuscany
       </a>
       , and has changed little in the last 700 years or so. Furthermore, the Tuscan dialect is the most conservative of all
       <a href="/wiki/Regional_Italian" title="Regional Italian">
        Italian dialects
       </a>
       , radically different from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallo-Italian_languages" title="Gallo-Italian languages">
        Gallo-Italian languages
       </a>
       less than 100 miles to the north (across the
       <a href="/wiki/La_Spezia%E2%80%93Rimini_Line" title="La Spezia–Rimini Line">
        La Spezia–Rimini Line
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The following are some of the conservative phonological features of Italian, as compared with the common
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Romance" title="Western Romance">
        Western Romance
       </a>
       languages (French, Spanish,
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">
        Galician
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       ). Some of these features are also present in
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
        Romanian
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Little or no
        <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">
         lenition
        </a>
        of consonants between vowels, e.g.
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         vīta
        </span>
        &gt;
        <i>
         vita
        </i>
        "life" (cf. Romanian
        <i>
         viață
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         vida
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [βiða]
        </span>
        , French
        <i>
         vie
        </i>
        ),
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         pedem
        </span>
        &gt;
        <i>
         piede
        </i>
        "foot" (cf. Spanish
        <i>
         pie
        </i>
        , French
        <i>
         pied
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /pje/
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Preservation of doubled consonants, e.g.
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         annum
        </span>
        &gt;
        <i>
         anno
        </i>
        "year" (cf. Spanish
        <i>
         año
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /aɲo/
        </span>
        , French
        <i>
         an
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɑ̃/
        </span>
        , Portuguese
        <i>
         ano
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ˈã.nu/
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Preservation of all
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Romance" title="Proto-Romance">
         Proto-Romance
        </a>
        final vowels, e.g.
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         pacem
        </span>
        &gt;
        <i>
         pace
        </i>
        "peace" (cf. Romanian
        <i>
         pace
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         paz
        </i>
        , French
        <i>
         paix
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /pɛ/
        </span>
        ),
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         octō
        </span>
        &gt;
        <i>
         otto
        </i>
        "eight" (cf. Romanian
        <i>
         opt
        </i>
        Spanish
        <i>
         ocho
        </i>
        , French
        <i>
         huit
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɥi(t)/
        </span>
        ),
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         fēcī
        </span>
        &gt;
        <i>
         feci
        </i>
        "I did" (cf. Spanish
        <i>
         hice
        </i>
        , French
        <i>
         fis
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /fi/
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Preservation of most intertonic vowels (those between the stressed syllable and either the beginning or ending syllable). This accounts for some of the most noticeable differences, as in the forms
        <i>
         quattordici
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         settimana
        </i>
        given above.
       </li>
       <li>
        Slower consonant development, e.g.
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         folia
        </span>
        &gt; Italo-Western
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /fɔʎʎa/
        </span>
        &gt;
        <i>
         foglia
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ˈfɔʎʎa/
        </span>
        "leaf" (cf. Romanian
        <i>
         foaie
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ˈfo̯aje/
        </span>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         hoja
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ˈoxa/
        </span>
        , French
        <i>
         feuille
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ˈfœj/
        </span>
        ; but note Portuguese
        <i>
         folha
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ˈfoʎɐ/
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Compared with most other Romance languages, Italian has a large number of inconsistent outcomes, where the same underlying sound produces different results in different words, e.g.
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        laxāre
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        lasciare
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        lassare
       </i>
       ,
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        captiāre
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        cacciare
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        cazzare
       </i>
       ,
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        (ex)dēroteolāre
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        sdrucciolare
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        druzzolare
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ruzzolare
       </i>
       ,
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        rēgīna
       </span>
       &gt;
       <i>
        regina
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        reina
       </i>
       ,
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        -c-
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /k/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɡ/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        -t-
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /d/
       </span>
       . Although in all these examples the second form has fallen out of usage, the dimorphism is thought to reflect the several-hundred-year period during which Italian developed as a literary language divorced from any native-speaking population, with an origin in 12th/13th-century Tuscan but with many words borrowed from
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
        languages
       </a>
       farther to the north, with different sound outcomes. (The
       <a href="/wiki/La_Spezia%E2%80%93Rimini_Line" title="La Spezia–Rimini Line">
        La Spezia–Rimini Line
       </a>
       , the most important
       <a href="/wiki/Isogloss" title="Isogloss">
        isogloss
       </a>
       in the entire Romance-language area, passes only about 20 miles to the north of Florence.)
      </p>
      <p>
       Some other features that distinguish Italian from the Western Romance languages:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Latin
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         ce-,ci-
        </span>
        becomes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʃe, tʃi/
        </span>
        rather than
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /(t)se, (t)si/
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Latin
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         -ct-
        </span>
        becomes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tt/
        </span>
        rather than
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /jt/
        </span>
        or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʃ/
        </span>
        :
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         octō
        </span>
        &gt;
        <i>
         otto
        </i>
        "eight" (cf. Spanish
        <i>
         ocho
        </i>
        , French
        <i>
         huit,
        </i>
        Portuguese
        <i>
         oito
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Vulgar Latin
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         -cl-
        </span>
        becomes
        <i>
         cchi
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /kkj/
        </span>
        rather than
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʎ/
        </span>
        :
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         oclum
        </span>
        &gt;
        <i>
         occhio
        </i>
        "eye" (cf. Portuguese
        <i>
         olho
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /oʎu/
        </span>
        , French
        <i>
         oeil
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /œj/
        </span>
        &lt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /œʎ/
        </span>
        ); but Romanian
        <i>
         ochi
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /okʲ/
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Final
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
        is not preserved, and vowel changes rather than
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
        are used to mark the plural:
        <i>
         amico, amici
        </i>
        "male friend(s)",
        <i>
         amica, amiche
        </i>
        "female friend(s)" (cf. Romanian
        <i>
         amic, amici
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         amică, amice
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         amigo(s)
        </i>
        "male friend(s)",
        <i>
         amiga(s)
        </i>
        "female friend(s)");
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         trēs, sex
        </span>
        →
        <i>
         tre, sei
        </i>
        "three, six" (cf. Romanian
        <i>
         trei, șase
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         tres, seis
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Standard Italian also differs in some respects from most nearby Italian languages:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Perhaps most noticeable is the total lack of
        <a href="/wiki/Metaphony_(Romance_languages)" title="Metaphony (Romance languages)">
         metaphony
        </a>
        , though metaphony is a feature characterizing nearly every other
        <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Italy" title="Languages of Italy">
         Italian language
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        No simplification of original
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /nd/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /mb/
        </span>
        (which often became
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /nn/, /mm/
        </span>
        elsewhere).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Assimilation">
        Assimilation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Assimilation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Italian
       <a href="/wiki/Phonotactics" title="Phonotactics">
        phonotactics
       </a>
       do not usually permit verbs and polysyllabic nouns to end with consonants, excepting poetry and song, so foreign words may receive
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epenthetic_vowel#Epenthesis_of_a_vowel,_or_anaptyxis" title="Epenthetic vowel">
        extra terminal vowel sounds
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">
        Writing system
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Writing system">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_alphabet" title="Italian alphabet">
        Italian alphabet
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Italian alphabet is typically considered to consist of 21 letters. The letters j, k, w, x, y are traditionally excluded, though they appear in loanwords such as
       <i>
        jeans
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        whisky
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        taxi
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        xenofobo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        xilofono
       </i>
       . The letter ⟨x⟩ has become common in standard Italian with the prefix
       <i>
        extra-
       </i>
       , although
       <i>
        (e)stra-
       </i>
       is traditionally used; it is also common to use of the Latin particle
       <i>
        ex(-)
       </i>
       to mean "former(ly)" as in:
       <i>
        la mia ex
       </i>
       ("my ex-girlfriend"), "Ex-Jugoslavia" ("Former Yugoslavia"). The letter ⟨j⟩ appears in the first name
       <i>
        Jacopo
       </i>
       and in some Italian place-names, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Bajardo" title="Bajardo">
        Bajardo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bojano" title="Bojano">
        Bojano
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Joppolo" title="Joppolo">
        Joppolo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jerzu" title="Jerzu">
        Jerzu
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jesolo" title="Jesolo">
        Jesolo
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesi" title="Jesi">
        Jesi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ajaccio" title="Ajaccio">
        Ajaccio
       </a>
       , among others, and in
       <i>
        Mar Jonio
       </i>
       , an alternative spelling of
       <i>
        Mar Ionio
       </i>
       (the
       <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Sea" title="Ionian Sea">
        Ionian Sea
       </a>
       ). The letter ⟨j⟩ may appear in dialectal words, but its use is discouraged in contemporary standard Italian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clivio_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Clivio-72">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Letters used in Foreign words can be replaced with
       <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">
        phonetically
       </a>
       equivalent native Italian letters and
       <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">
        digraphs
       </a>
       : ⟨gi⟩, ⟨ge⟩, or ⟨i⟩ for ⟨j⟩; ⟨c⟩ or ⟨ch⟩ for ⟨k⟩ (including in the standard prefix
       <i>
        kilo-
       </i>
       ); ⟨o⟩, ⟨u⟩ or ⟨v⟩ for ⟨w⟩; ⟨s⟩, ⟨ss⟩, ⟨z⟩, ⟨zz⟩ or ⟨cs⟩ for ⟨x⟩; and ⟨e⟩ or ⟨i⟩ for ⟨y⟩.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">
         acute accent
        </a>
        is used over word-final ⟨e⟩ to indicate a stressed
        <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">
         front close-mid vowel
        </a>
        , as in
        <i>
         perché
        </i>
        "why, because". In dictionaries, it is also used over ⟨o⟩ to indicate a stressed
        <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back rounded vowel">
         back close-mid vowel
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         azióne
        </i>
        ). The
        <a href="/wiki/Grave_accent" title="Grave accent">
         grave accent
        </a>
        is used over word-final ⟨e⟩ to indicate a
        <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">
         front open-mid vowel
        </a>
        , as in
        <i>
         tè
        </i>
        "tea". The grave accent is used over any vowel to indicate word-final stress, as in
        <i>
         gioventù
        </i>
        "youth". Unlike ⟨é⟩, a stressed final ⟨o⟩ is always a
        <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">
         back open-mid vowel
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         andrò
        </i>
        ), making ⟨ó⟩ unnecessary outside of dictionaries. Most of the time, the penultimate syllable is stressed. But if the stressed vowel is the final letter of the word, the accent is mandatory, otherwise it is virtually always omitted. Exceptions are typically either in dictionaries, where all or most stressed vowels are commonly marked. Accents can optionally be used disambiguate words that differ only by stress, as for
        <i>
         prìncipi
        </i>
        "princes" and
        <i>
         princìpi
        </i>
        "principles", or
        <i>
         àncora
        </i>
        "anchor" and
        <i>
         ancóra
        </i>
        "still
        <i>
         /
        </i>
        yet". For monosyllabic words, the rule is different: when two identical monosyllabic words with different meanings exist, one is accented and the other is not (example:
        <i>
         è
        </i>
        "is",
        <i>
         e
        </i>
        "and").
       </li>
       <li>
        The letter ⟨h⟩ distinguishes
        <i>
         ho
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         hai
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         ha
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         hanno
        </i>
        (present indicative of
        <i>
         avere
        </i>
        "to have") from
        <i>
         o
        </i>
        ("or"),
        <i>
         ai
        </i>
        ("to the"),
        <i>
         a
        </i>
        ("to"),
        <i>
         anno
        </i>
        ("year"). In the spoken language, the letter is always silent. The ⟨h⟩ in
        <i>
         ho
        </i>
        additionally marks the contrasting open pronunciation of the ⟨o⟩. The letter ⟨h⟩ is also used in combinations with other letters. No
        <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
         phoneme
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [h]
        </span>
        exists in Italian. In nativized foreign words, the ⟨h⟩ is silent. For example,
        <i>
         hotel
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         hovercraft
        </i>
        are pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /oˈtɛl/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ˈɔverkraft/
        </span>
        respectively. (Where ⟨h⟩ existed in Latin, it either disappeared or, in a few cases before a back vowel, changed to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɡ]
        </span>
        :
        <i>
         traggo
        </i>
        "I pull" ← Lat.
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
         trahō
        </span>
        .)
       </li>
       <li>
        The letters ⟨s⟩ and ⟨z⟩ can symbolize
        <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">
         voiced
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Voicelessness" title="Voicelessness">
         voiceless
        </a>
        consonants. ⟨z⟩ symbolizes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /dz/
        </span>
        or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ts/
        </span>
        depending on context, with few minimal pairs. For example:
        <i>
         zanzara
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /dzanˈdzaːra/
        </span>
        "mosquito" and
        <i>
         nazione
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /natˈtsjoːne/
        </span>
        "nation". ⟨s⟩ symbolizes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
        word-initially before a vowel, when clustered with a voiceless consonant (⟨p, f, c, ch⟩), and when doubled; it symbolizes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /z/
        </span>
        when between vowels and when clustered with voiced consonants. Intervocalic ⟨s⟩ varies regionally between
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /z/
        </span>
        , with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /z/
        </span>
        being more dominant in northern Italy and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
        in the south.
       </li>
       <li>
        The letters ⟨c⟩ and ⟨g⟩ vary in pronunciation between
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plosives" title="Plosives">
         plosives
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affricates" title="Affricates">
         affricates
        </a>
        depending on following vowels. The letter ⟨c⟩ symbolizes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /k/
        </span>
        when word-final and before the back vowels ⟨a, o, u⟩. It symbolizes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_palato-alveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless palato-alveolar affricate">
          tʃ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        as in
        <i>
         chair
        </i>
        before the front vowels ⟨e, i⟩. The letter ⟨g⟩ symbolizes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɡ/
        </span>
        when word-final and before the back vowels ⟨a, o, u⟩. It symbolizes
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_affricate" title="Voiced palato-alveolar affricate">
          dʒ
         </a>
         /
        </span>
        as in
        <i>
         gem
        </i>
        before the front vowels ⟨e, i⟩. Other Romance languages and, to an extent, English have similar variations for ⟨c, g⟩. Compare
        <a href="/wiki/Hard_and_soft_C" title="Hard and soft C">
         hard and soft C
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Hard_and_soft_G" title="Hard and soft G">
         hard and soft G
        </a>
        . (See also
        <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">
         palatalization
        </a>
        .)
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">
         digraphs
        </a>
        ⟨ch⟩ and ⟨gh⟩ indicate or preserve hardness (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /k/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɡ/
        </span>
        ) before ⟨i, e⟩. The digraphs ⟨ci⟩ and ⟨gi⟩ indicate or preserve softness (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʃ/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /dʒ/
        </span>
        ) before ⟨a, o, u⟩. For example:
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <table class="wikitable">
         <tr>
          <th>
          </th>
          <th colspan="2">
           Before back vowel (A, O, U)
          </th>
          <th colspan="2">
           Before front vowel (I, E)
          </th>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <th rowspan="2">
           Plosive
          </th>
          <th>
           C
          </th>
          <td>
           caramella
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /karaˈmɛlla/
           </span>
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Candy" title="Candy">
             candy
            </a>
           </i>
          </td>
          <th>
           CH
          </th>
          <td>
           china
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /ˈkiːna/
           </span>
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/India_ink" title="India ink">
             India ink
            </a>
           </i>
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <th>
           G
          </th>
          <td>
           gallo
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /ˈɡallo/
           </span>
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Rooster" title="Rooster">
             rooster
            </a>
           </i>
          </td>
          <th>
           GH
          </th>
          <td>
           ghiro
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /ˈɡiːro/
           </span>
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Edible_dormouse" title="Edible dormouse">
             edible dormouse
            </a>
           </i>
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <th rowspan="2">
           Affricate
          </th>
          <th>
           CI
          </th>
          <td>
           ciambella
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /tʃambɛlla/
           </span>
           <i>
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donut" title="Donut">
             donut
            </a>
           </i>
          </td>
          <th>
           C
          </th>
          <td>
           Cina
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /ˈtʃiːna/
           </span>
           <i>
            China
           </i>
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <th>
           GI
          </th>
          <td>
           giallo
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /ˈdʒallo/
           </span>
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Yellow" title="Yellow">
             yellow
            </a>
           </i>
          </td>
          <th>
           G
          </th>
          <td>
           giro
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
